Concepts

2.1 Meteorite and other extra-terrestrial rock and sediment

  • Includes meteorites, Lunar rock and sediment, and rock and sediment from other extraterrestrial bodies.

  • Source: https://www.mindat.org/min-49504.html

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49504:6

2.1.1 Lunar igneous rock

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49504:6

  • Primary lunar igneous rocks.

  • Source: https://www.mindat.org/min-49505.html

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49505:5

2.1.1.1 Mare basalt

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49505:5

  • Basalt from the lunar maria.

  • Source: https://www.mindat.org/min-49506.html

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49506:4

2.1.1.2 Highland rock

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49505:5

  • Older, highland lunar rock.

  • Source: https://www.mindat.org/min-49507.html

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49507:3

2.1.1.2.1 Lunar granophyric granite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:48144:1 etma:1:1:49507:3

  • Fragments of this material have been found in lunar breccias and soils.

  • Source: https://www.mindat.org/min-48145.html

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:48145:0

2.1.2 Lunar fragmental rock and sediment

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49504:6

  • Lunar rock and soils composed of fragments of older rocks.

  • Source: https://www.mindat.org/min-49508.html

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49508:2

2.1.2.1 Lunar breccia

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49508:2

  • Lunar rocks composed of sharp angular fragments of older rocks, often including reworked fragments of previous lunar breccias.

  • Source: https://www.mindat.org/min-49509.html

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49509:1

2.1.2.1.1 Shock lithified impact regolith

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49089:2 etma:1:1:49509:1

  • Consolidated clastic impact debris.

  • Source: https://www.mindat.org/min-49091.html

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49091:7

2.1.2.1.1.1 Shock lithified impact regolith breccia

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49091:7 etma:1:1:50222:1

  • Consolidated clastic impact debris breccia.

  • Source: https://www.mindat.org/min-51453.html

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:51453:6

2.1.2.1.2 Feldspathic breccia

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49509:1

  • A lunar breccia rich in feldspar

  • Source: https://www.mindat.org/min-49510.html

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49510:7

2.1.2.2 Lunar regolith

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49508:2

  • Lunar soil.

  • Source: https://www.mindat.org/min-49511.html

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49511:6

2.1.2.2.1 Lunar highland regolith

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49511:6

  • A lunar soil from the lunar highlands. Commonly contains felspathic breccias, agglutinates, feldspar framents and occasional mare basalt fragments.

  • Source: https://www.mindat.org/min-49512.html

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49512:5

2.1.2.2.2 Lunar mare regolith

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49511:6

  • A lunar soil from the lunar maria (dark basaltic plains.) Mostly derived from High-Ti basalts.

  • Source: https://www.mindat.org/min-49513.html

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49513:4

2.1.3 Meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49504:6

  • Rock types associated with meteorites and asteroids. Meteorite Mineralogy: An Explicitly Planetological-Planetochemical Perspective

  • Source: https://www.mindat.org/min-49514.html

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49514:3

2.1.3.1 Chondrite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49514:3

  • A primitive meteorite, usually bearing chondrules, that has not experienced igneous differentiation on its parent body.

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=Chondrite-uncl

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49515:2

2.1.3.1.1 Chondrite-fusion crust meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49515:2

  • A piece of fusion crust that became detatched from a chondrite.

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=Chondrite-fusion%20crust, https://www.mindat.org/min-49517.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49517:0

2.1.3.1.2 Ungrouped chondrite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49515:2

  • An ungrouped chondrite.

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=Chondrite-ung, https://www.mindat.org/min-49518.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49518:9

2.1.3.1.3 Carbonaceous chondrite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49515:2

  • Stony (non-metallic) meteorites that have not been modified due to melting or differentiation of the parent body. They include some of the most primitive known meteorites. The C chondrites represent only a small proportion (4.6 percent ) of meteorite falls.

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=C, https://www.mindat.org/min-49519.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49519:8

2.1.3.1.3.1 C chondrite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49519:8

  • Undifferentiated carbonaceous chondrites.

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=C, https://www.mindat.org/min-49520.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49520:4

2.1.3.1.3.1.1 C1 chondrite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49520:4 etma:1:1:49840:5

  • A C chondrite that is petrologic type 1.

  • Source: https://www.mindat.org/min-49521.html

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49521:3

2.1.3.1.3.1.2 Ungrouped C1 chondrite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49521:3

  • A C chondrite that is petrologic type 1, ungrouped.

  • Alternate labels: C1-ung chondrite meteorite

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=C1-ung, https://www.mindat.org/min-49522.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49522:2

2.1.3.1.3.1.3 Ungrouped C1/2 chondrite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49521:3 etma:1:1:49524:0

  • Alternate labels: C1/2-ung chondrite meteorite

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=C1/2-ung, https://www.mindat.org/min-49523.html,

2.1.3.1.3.8 CR chondrite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49519:8

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=CR, https://www.mindat.org/min-49571.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49571:8

2.1.3.1.3.9 CV chondrite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49519:8

  • The Vigarano (CV) chemical group of carbonaceous chondrites, distinguished by large (mm-sized) chondrules, many of which are surrounded by igneous rims, large refractory inclusions and abundant matrix (40 volume percent); CV chondrites may be divided into oxidized and reduced subgroups.

  • Source: https://www.mindat.org/min-49577.html

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49577:2

2.1.3.1.4 Ordinary chondrite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49515:2

  • A major class of chondrites, distinguished by sub-solar Mg/Si and refractory/Si ratios, oxygen isotope compositions that plot above the terrestrial fractionation line, and a large volume percentage of chondrules, with only 10-15 volume percent fine-grained matrix.

  • Alternate labels: O chondrite meteorite, OC chondrite meteorite,

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=OC, https://www.mindat.org/min-49581.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49581:5

2.1.3.1.4.1 H chondrite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49581:5

  • The high-iron (H) chemical group of ordinary chondrites, distinguished by their high siderophile element content, relatively small chondrules (~0.3 mm), and oxygen isotope compositions that are closer to the terrestrial fractionation line than those of other ordinary chondrites.

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=H, https://www.mindat.org/min-49584.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49584:2

2.1.3.1.4.2 L chondrite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49581:5

  • The low-iron (L) chemical group of ordinary chondrites, distinguished by their relatively low siderophile element content, moderate sized chondrules (~0.7 mm), and oxygen isotope compositions that intermediate between H chondrites and LL chondrites. Parent body is possibly 433 Eros, 8 Flora or the Flora family as a whole

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=L, https://www.mindat.org/min-49648.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49648:9

2.1.3.1.4.3 LL chondrite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49581:5

  • The low-iron, low metal (LL) chemical group of ordinary chondrites, distinguished by their low siderophile element content, fairly large chondrules (~0.9 mm), and oxygen isotope compositions that are further above the terrestrial fractionation line than those of other ordinary chondrites.

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=LL, https://www.mindat.org/min-49745.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49745:1

2.1.3.1.4.3.85 LL7 chondrite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49745:1

  • An ordinary chondrite from the LL group that is petrologic type 7. Note: Type 7 designates chondrites which have been metamorphosed to nearly the point of melting. Minerals are equilibrated and chondrules are indistinct or absent. This term is not used consistently or widely accepted in the literature.

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=LL7, https://www.mindat.org/min-52394.html,

2.1.3.1.5 Enstatite chondrite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49515:2

  • A major class of chondrites that mostly have sub-solar Mg/Si and refractory/Si ratios, oxygen isotope compositions that plot near the terrestrial fractionation line, and highly reduced mineral assemblages (containing little FeO, Si-bearing metal, and sulfides of elements normally considered lithophile.

  • Source: https://www.mindat.org/min-49800.html

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49800:7

2.1.3.1.6 K chondrite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49515:2

  • A chondrite characterised by large amounts of dusty matrix and oxygen isotope compositions similar to carbonaceous chondrites, highly reduced mineral compositions and high metal abundances (6 percent to 10 percent by volume) that are most like enstatite chondrites, and concentrations of refractory lithophile elements that are most like ordinary chondrites.

  • Alternate labels: Kakangari chondrite meteorite

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=K, https://www.mindat.org/min-49829.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49829:2

2.1.3.1.7 R chondrite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49515:2

  • A chondrite, different from ordinary chondrite in having a more dusty matrix (about 50 percent of the rock), more oxidized, containing little metallic Fe-Ni and with a higher 17O ratio. Most of the metal is in the form of sulfides. They appear to come from an asteroid regolith.

  • Alternate labels: Rumuruti chondrite meteorite

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=R, https://www.mindat.org/min-49833.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49833:5

2.1.3.1.8 Chondrite meteorite by petrological type

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49515:2

  • An alternative classification grouping together all chondrite types by their petrological type rather than origin.

  • Source: https://www.mindat.org/min-49839.html

2.1.3.1.8.1 Petrologic Type 1 chondrite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49839:9

  • Chondrites which have experienced a high degree of aqueous alteration. Most primary minerals have been replaced by secondary phases and chondrules are generally absent.

2.1.3.1.8.3 Petrologic Type 3 chondrite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49839:9

  • Chondrites that are characterized by abundant chondrules, low degrees of aqueous alteration, and unequilibrated mineral assemblages. Many of the low-Ca pyroxene grains are monoclinic and exhibit polysynthetic twinning. The type 3 chondrites may be divided into subtypes ranging from 3.00 (least metamorphosed) to 3.9 (nearly metamorphosed to type 4 levels). If primary igneous glass occurs in the chondrules, it belongs to type 3.

  • Source: https://www.mindat.org/min-49842.html

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49842:3

2.1.3.1.8.4 Petrologic Type 4 chondrite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49839:9

  • Chondrites that are characterized by abundant chondrules, and have been metamorphosed under conditions sufficient to homogenize olivine compositions and recrystallize fine-grained matrix. Some of the low-Ca pyroxene grains may be monoclinic and exhibit polysynthetic twinning. Primary igneous chondrule glass is absent.

  • Source: https://www.mindat.org/min-49843.html

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49843:2

2.1.3.1.8.6 Petrologic Type 6 chondrite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49839:9

  • Chondrites that have been metamorphosed under conditions sufficient to homogenize all mineral compositions, convert all low-Ca pyroxene to orthopyroxene, coarsen secondary phases such as feldspar to sizes greater that or equal to 50 micron, and obliterate many chondrule outlines; no melting has occurred.

  • Source: https://www.mindat.org/min-49845.html

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49845:0

2.1.3.1.8.7 Petrologic Type 7 chondrite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49839:9

  • Chondrites which have been metamorphosed to nearly the point of melting. Minerals are equilibrated and chondrules are indistinct or absent. This term is not used consistently or widely accepted in the literature.

  • Source: https://www.mindat.org/min-49846.html

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49846:9

2.1.3.2 Primitive achondrite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49514:3

  • A meteorite that has lost its chondritic texture due to heating and partial melting, but still has nearly chondritic composition.

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=Achondrite-prim, https://www.mindat.org/min-49847.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49847:8

2.1.3.2.1 Lodranite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49847:8

  • Lodranites can be described as chondrites that were heated to the point where FeNi and FeS reach an eutectic point. At this point partial melting occurred and some of the metal and silica melt was removed.

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=Lodranite, https://www.mindat.org/min-11263.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:11263:9

2.1.3.2.1.1 Acapulcoite-lodranite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:11263:9 etma:1:1:49848:7

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=Acapulcoite/lodranite, https://www.mindat.org/min-49849.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49849:6

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49865:4

2.1.3.2.2 Acapulcoite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49847:8

  • Acapulcoites, named after the Acapulco, Mexico, fall of 1913, and lodranites, named after the Lodran, Pakistan, fall of 1868, are closely related, equigranular meteorites; acapulcoites are finer grained than lodranites and contain rare, relict chondrules, and there are transitional meteorites between the two types (e.g., EET 84302, GRA 95209). Mineral assemblages are similar to, but distinct from those of ordinary chondrites. Compositions are subchondritic, with lodranites showing a higher degree of fractionation.

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=Acapulcoite, https://www.mindat.org/min-49848.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49848:7

2.1.3.2.2.1 Acapulcoite-lodranite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:11263:9 etma:1:1:49848:7

  • A transitional type between acapulcoite and lodranite.

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=Acapulcoite/lodranite, https://www.mindat.org/min-49849.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49849:6

2.1.3.2.3 Brachinite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49847:8 etma:1:1:50270:8

  • Primitive achondrite meteorites almost entirely made from olivine; the parent body might be the Nenetta Asteroid (289 Nenetta). Named after Brachina, a meteorite found in Australia in 1974.

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=Brachinite, https://www.mindat.org/min-49850.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49850:2

2.1.3.2.6 Ureilite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49847:8

  • Ureilites, named after the Novo-Urei, Russia, fall of 1886, are ultramafic achondrites that contain interstitial carbon as graphite or diamond. The majority consist of olivine + uninverted pigeonite. In a few, the pyroxene is augite and/or orthopyroxene instead. In addition, about 10 percent of ureilites are polymict breccias, containing a few percent of feldspathic material in addition to typical ureilitic components.

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=Ureilite, https://www.mindat.org/min-49866.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49866:3

2.1.3.2.6.1 Anomalous ureilite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49866:3

  • An achondrite belonging to the ureilite group with anomalous properties.

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=Ureilite-an, https://www.mindat.org/min-49867.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49867:2

2.1.3.2.6.2 Polymict ureilite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49866:3

  • A stony meteorite that, originated on a differentiated parent body, lacks chondrules, belongs to the ureilite group, and is a polymict breccia

  • Alternate labels: Ureilite-pmict meteorite

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=Ureilite-pmict

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49868:1

2.1.3.2.7 Winonaite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49847:8

  • Winonaites, named after the Winona, Arizona, find of 1928, are equigranular rocks, some of which may contain relict chondrules, that have mineralogy and composition that are similar to chondrites.The mineral assemblage is more reduced than that of H chondrites. Winonaites are related to the silicate inclusions found in IAB complex irons.

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=Winonaite, https://www.mindat.org/min-49869.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49869:0

2.1.3.3.7 Catarinite

2.1.3.3.8 Ataxite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49960:8

  • Ataxites are a structural class of iron meteorites with a high nickel content and show no Widmanstatten patterns upon etching. Ataxites are the most nickel-rich meteorites known; they usually contain over 18 percent nickel. The high nickel content is the reason that they do not develop a Widmanstatten structure, because in this case kamacite can be exsolved from taenite only at such a low temperature (below about 600 degrees C) where diffusion is already too slow. Most ataxites belong to the IVB group or are classified as ‘Iron, ungrouped’ because they do not fit in any of the groups currently recognized by meteorite classification. Some ataxites belong to the IAB group and fall into the sHL (high-Au, low-Ni subgroup), sLH (low-Au, high-Ni subgroup), sHH (high-Au, high-Ni subgroup) and ‘ung’ subgroup (does not fit any subgroup of IAB). Only a couple of ataxites have been classified into the IAB complex and the IIF, IVA, IIAB, IIIAB groups.

  • Source: https://www.mindat.org/min-52787.html

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:52787:7

2.1.3.3.9 Octahedrite (meteorite)

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49960:8

  • Octahedrite is a historical classification for iron meteorites based on structure. It shows a Widmanstatten pattern of kamacite exsolution lamellae out of taenite. See also octahedrite (of Werner), a synonym of anatase.

  • Source: https://www.mindat.org/min-54116.html

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:54116:9

2.1.3.3.10 Hexahedrite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49960:8

  • An older structural classification of iron meteorites based on the absence of the Widmanstatten pattern, which can be assessed from the appearance of polished cross-sections that have been etched with acid. They have a low abundance of nickel relative to iron.

  • Source: https://www.mindat.org/min-54117.html

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:54117:8

2.1.3.4 Stony-iron meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49514:3

  • meteorites that consist of nearly equal parts of meteoric iron and silicates

  • Source: https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stony-iron_meteorite

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49963:5

2.1.3.4.1 Pallasite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49963:5 etma:1:1:50269:2

  • A stony-iron meteorite composed essentially of large single glassy crystals of olivine (forsterite) embedded in a network of nickel-iron, with accessory troilite, chromite, phosphide, various phosphate minerals, and sometimes pyroxene. Pallasites are believed to have been formed at the interface of the stony mantle and metal core of layered planetoids. For a long time, pallasites have been divided into two groups, Main Group (MGP) and the Eagle Station grouplet (PES), based on their oxygen isotopic signatures and forsterite compositions. However, with the new millennium came not only new pallasite samples, but new pallasite subtypes. Thus, in only a few years, the number of pallasite parent bodies has increased from two to five isotopically and/or mineralogically distinct objects, with Scott (2007) suggesting as many as seven.

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=Pallasite, https://www.mindat.org/min-49898.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49898:2

2.1.3.4.1.1 PMG pallasite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49898:2

  • A pallasite belonging to the main chemical group. Metal compositions in all PMG are closely related to those in evolved IIIAB irons, and are generally consistent with their formation in the IIIAB parent asteroid. The O-isotope composition of their olivine (forsterite) is a main parameter to separate the main-group pallasites (PMG) and Eagle- Station pallasites (PES). The mean PMG delta 17O (= delta17O - 0.52(delta 18O)) value is -0.28 +/- 0.06 per mil. The mean PES delta17O value is much lower, about -4.6 per mil. The major mineral assemblages of most PMG are quite simple, consisting of troilite and schreibersite in addition to olivine (forsterite), kamacite and taenite; olivine Fa values range from 11 to 18 mole percent , with greater than 80 percent of the values in the range 11.6-13.5 mole percent (Buseck and Goldstein, 1969). Zaisho (Fa18.3) has the highest Fa among PMG (Buseck and Clark, 1984). In addition, several characteristic minor minerals have been reported. Chromite is relatively abundant; some grains have euhedral shapes and seem to have crystallised from a melt (Buseck, 1977). A few chromites are quite massive; a large (10-cm) chromite-olivine assemblage in Brenham was described in detail by Wasson et al. (1999). Phosphates are minor phases in all PMG (common only in Springwater); the identified phosphates are farringtonite, stanfieldite, and merrillite (Buseck and Holdsworth, 1977). Phosphoran olivine is rare in most pallasites but minor amounts may be ubiquitous in the more oxidized PMG (Buseck, 1977; Buseck and Clark, 1984). Low-Ca pyroxene is a rare accessory phase (Buseck, 1977). Some PMG are assigned to an anomalous subset (PMG-an) because of their anomalous properties (deviant metal compositions or appreciably higher olivine Fa contents).

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=Pallasite,%20PMG, https://www.mindat.org/min-49899.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49899:1

2.1.3.4.1.1.1 Anomalous PMG pallasite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49899:1

  • A PMG pallasite with anomalous properties (deviant metal composition or appreciably higher olivine Fa content).

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=Pallasite,%20PMG-an, https://www.mindat.org/min-49901.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49901:5

2.1.3.4.1.2 PES pallasite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49898:2

  • A grouplet named for a pallasite that was found near Eagle Station, Kentucky, in 1880. They all contain highly fragmented olivines, intermixed with small, irregular olivine splinters, in a nickel-iron matrix. Although their mineral assemblage is similar to PMG pallasites, they have different metal and olivine (forsterite) compositions. In fact, the mean Ni content of the metal is higher, and olivine Fa contents are slightly higher (about Fa20) than those in the Zaisho (Fa18.3), which has the highest Fa among PMG. The O-isotope composition of their olivine (forsterite) is a main parameter to separate the Eagle-Station pallasites (PES) and main-group pallasites (PMG). The mean PMG delta 17O (= delta17O - 0.52 delta18O) value is -0.28 +/- 0.06 per mil. The mean PES delta 17O value is much lower, about -4.6 per mil.

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=Pallasite,%20PES, https://www.mindat.org/min-49902.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49902:4

2.1.3.4.1.3 Pyroxene pallasite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49898:2

  • A pallasite containing pyroxene as inclusions in the olivine crystals, as large grains in the nickel-iron matrix and as grains bordering the olivines.

  • Source: https://www.mindat.org/min-49904.html

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49904:2

2.1.3.4.1.4 Ungrouped pallasite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49898:2

  • A pallasite that is neither a member of the main group nor the Eagle Station grouplet, and is therefore ungrouped.

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=Pallasite,%20ungrouped, https://www.mindat.org/min-52371.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:52371:0

2.1.3.5 Differentiated achondrite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49514:3

  • Source: https://www.mindat.org/min-50269.html

2.1.3.5.1 Ungrouped enstatite-rich achondrite meteorite

2.1.3.5.2 Pallasite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49963:5 etma:1:1:50269:2

  • A stony-iron meteorite composed essentially of large single glassy crystals of olivine (forsterite) embedded in a network of nickel-iron, with accessory troilite, chromite, phosphide, various phosphate minerals, and sometimes pyroxene. Pallasites are believed to have been formed at the interface of the stony mantle and metal core of layered planetoids. For a long time, pallasites have been divided into two groups, Main Group (MGP) and the Eagle Station grouplet (PES), based on their oxygen isotopic signatures and forsterite compositions. However, with the new millennium came not only new pallasite samples, but new pallasite subtypes. Thus, in only a few years, the number of pallasite parent bodies has increased from two to five isotopically and/or mineralogically distinct objects, with Scott (2007) suggesting as many as seven.

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=Pallasite, https://www.mindat.org/min-49898.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49898:2

2.1.3.5.2.1 PMG pallasite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49898:2

  • A pallasite belonging to the main chemical group. Metal compositions in all PMG are closely related to those in evolved IIIAB irons, and are generally consistent with their formation in the IIIAB parent asteroid. The O-isotope composition of their olivine (forsterite) is a main parameter to separate the main-group pallasites (PMG) and Eagle- Station pallasites (PES). The mean PMG delta 17O (= delta17O - 0.52(delta 18O)) value is -0.28 +/- 0.06 per mil. The mean PES delta17O value is much lower, about -4.6 per mil. The major mineral assemblages of most PMG are quite simple, consisting of troilite and schreibersite in addition to olivine (forsterite), kamacite and taenite; olivine Fa values range from 11 to 18 mole percent , with greater than 80 percent of the values in the range 11.6-13.5 mole percent (Buseck and Goldstein, 1969). Zaisho (Fa18.3) has the highest Fa among PMG (Buseck and Clark, 1984). In addition, several characteristic minor minerals have been reported. Chromite is relatively abundant; some grains have euhedral shapes and seem to have crystallised from a melt (Buseck, 1977). A few chromites are quite massive; a large (10-cm) chromite-olivine assemblage in Brenham was described in detail by Wasson et al. (1999). Phosphates are minor phases in all PMG (common only in Springwater); the identified phosphates are farringtonite, stanfieldite, and merrillite (Buseck and Holdsworth, 1977). Phosphoran olivine is rare in most pallasites but minor amounts may be ubiquitous in the more oxidized PMG (Buseck, 1977; Buseck and Clark, 1984). Low-Ca pyroxene is a rare accessory phase (Buseck, 1977). Some PMG are assigned to an anomalous subset (PMG-an) because of their anomalous properties (deviant metal compositions or appreciably higher olivine Fa contents).

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=Pallasite,%20PMG, https://www.mindat.org/min-49899.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49899:1

2.1.3.5.2.1.1 Anomalous PMG pallasite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49899:1

  • A PMG pallasite with anomalous properties (deviant metal composition or appreciably higher olivine Fa content).

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=Pallasite,%20PMG-an, https://www.mindat.org/min-49901.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49901:5

2.1.3.5.2.2 PES pallasite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49898:2

  • A grouplet named for a pallasite that was found near Eagle Station, Kentucky, in 1880. They all contain highly fragmented olivines, intermixed with small, irregular olivine splinters, in a nickel-iron matrix. Although their mineral assemblage is similar to PMG pallasites, they have different metal and olivine (forsterite) compositions. In fact, the mean Ni content of the metal is higher, and olivine Fa contents are slightly higher (about Fa20) than those in the Zaisho (Fa18.3), which has the highest Fa among PMG. The O-isotope composition of their olivine (forsterite) is a main parameter to separate the Eagle-Station pallasites (PES) and main-group pallasites (PMG). The mean PMG delta 17O (= delta17O - 0.52 delta18O) value is -0.28 +/- 0.06 per mil. The mean PES delta 17O value is much lower, about -4.6 per mil.

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=Pallasite,%20PES, https://www.mindat.org/min-49902.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49902:4

  • A pallasite containing pyroxene as inclusions in the olivine crystals, as large grains in the nickel-iron matrix and as grains bordering the olivines.

  • Primitive achondrite meteorites almost entirely made from olivine; the parent body might be the Nenetta Asteroid (289 Nenetta). Named after Brachina, a meteorite found in Australia in 1974.

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=Brachinite, https://www.mindat.org/min-49850.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49850:2

2.1.3.5.6.2 Angrite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:50270:8

  • Angrites, named after the Angra dos Reis, Brazil, fall of 1869, are a relatively rare type of basaltic achondrite with low alkali contents, high Ca/Al ratios, and a distinctive mineral assemblage, generally including Al-Ca-Ti-rich pyroxene, Ca-rich plagioclase, and Ca-rich olivine that may have exsolved the mineral kirschsteinite.

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=Angrite, https://www.mindat.org/min-49872.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49872:4

2.1.3.5.6.3 Aubrite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:50270:8

  • Aubrites, named after the Aubres, France, fall of 1836, are enstatite achondrites. Most are breccias (Shallowater being a notable exception) containing igneous and impact-melted clasts. Like enstatite chondrites, aubrites have highly reduced mineralogy, but they probably do not come from the same parent asteroid as either EH or EL chondrites.

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=Aubrite, https://www.mindat.org/min-49873.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49873:3

2.1.3.5.6.3.1 Anomalous aubrite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49873:3

  • An aubrite with certain anomalous properties.

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=Aubrite-an, https://www.mindat.org/min-52372.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:52372:9

2.1.3.5.6.4 HED achondrite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:50270:8

  • A group of meteorites believed to have come from asteroid 4 Vesta.

  • Source: https://www.mindat.org/min-49877.html

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49877:9

2.1.3.5.6.4.1 Howardite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49877:9

  • Howardites are an abundant group of polymict-breccia achondrites that appear to represent mixtures of eucrites and diogenites (these three linked groups are collectively known as HED meteorites and may come from asteroid 4 Vesta). The main minerals in howardites are pyroxene (largely orthopyroxene) and Na-poor plagioclase. A minority of howardites are rich in solar-wind noble gases and thus inferred to be regolith breccias.

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=Howardite, https://www.mindat.org/min-49878.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49878:8

2.1.3.5.6.4.2 Anomalous howardite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49878:8

  • An howardite that has anomalous properties.

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=Howardite-an, https://www.mindat.org/min-49879.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49879:7

2.1.3.5.6.4.3 Eucrite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49877:9

  • Eucrites are the most abundant type of basaltic achondrite, linked by geochemical traits such as oxygen isotopic ratios and certain elemental ratios, of which Fe/Mn is the most widely cited. The main minerals in eucrites are Fe-rich pyroxene and Na-poor plagioclase. The eucrites are strongly linked with the diogenites and howardites; the three groups are collectively known as HED meteorites and may come from asteroid 4 Vesta. Note that the name eucrite was previously improperly applied to varieties of gabbro, usually ophitic, where the plagioclase was bytownite with both ortho- and clino-pyroxenes and olivine. An important rock type in the Tertiary complexes of Scotland, UK. This term should no longer be used as a rock name.

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=Eucrite, https://www.mindat.org/min-49880.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49880:3

  • An anomalous Eucrite.

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49881:2

2.1.3.5.6.4.12 Diogenite meteorite

  • Child of: etma:1:1:49877:9

  • Diogenites are an abundant type of achondrite, linked by geochemical traits such as oxygen isotopic ratios and certain elemental ratios, of which Fe/Mn is the most widely cited. The dominant mineral in diogenites is orthopyroxene. The diogenites are strongly linked with two other achondrite groups: eucrites and howardites; the three groups are collectively known as HED meteorites and may come from asteroid 4 Vesta.

  • Source: https://www.lpi.usra.edu/meteor/metbullclass.php?sea=Diogenite, https://www.mindat.org/min-49889.html,

  • Concept URI token: etma:1:1:49889:4
